<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Beak</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Beak_(disambiguation)" title="Beak (disambiguation)">Beak (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BirdBeaksA.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="465" height="662" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/BirdBeaksA.svg/220px-BirdBeaksA.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/BirdBeaksA.svg/330px-BirdBeaksA.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/BirdBeaksA.svg/440px-BirdBeaksA.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BirdBeaksA.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Comparison of bird beaks, displaying different shapes adapted to different feeding methods. Not to scale.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>beak</b>, <b>bill</b>, or <b>rostrum</b> is an external anatomical structure of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">birds</a> that is used for eating and for <a href="/wiki/Personal_grooming#In_animals" title="Personal grooming">grooming</a>, manipulating objects, killing prey, fighting, probing for food, <a href="/wiki/Courtship#Courtship_in_the_animal_kingdom" title="Courtship">courtship</a> and feeding young. The terms <i>beak</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rostrum_(anatomy)" title="Rostrum (anatomy)">rostrum</a></i> are also used to refer to a similar mouth part in some <a href="/wiki/Dicynodont" title="Dicynodont">dicynodonts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ornithischians" title="Ornithischians">Ornithischians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod_beak" title="Cephalopod beak">cephalopods</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetacean" title="Cetacean">cetaceans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billfish" title="Billfish">billfishes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tetraodontidae" title="Tetraodontidae">pufferfishes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anuran" title="Anuran">Anuran</a> <a href="/wiki/Tadpole" title="Tadpole">tadpoles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sirenidae" title="Sirenidae">sirens</a>.</p>
<p>Although beaks vary significantly in size, shape, color and texture, they share a similar underlying structure. Two bony projections—the upper and lower mandibles—are covered with a thin keratinized layer of epidermis known as the rhamphotheca. In most species, two holes known as nares lead to the respiratory system.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although the word <i>beak</i> was, in the past, generally restricted to the sharpened bills of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partington_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Partington-1">[1]</a></sup> in modern <a href="/wiki/Ornithology" title="Ornithology">ornithology</a>, the terms <i>beak</i> and <i>bill</i> are generally considered to be synonymous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Proctor66_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Proctor66-2">[2]</a></sup> The word, which dates from the 13th century, comes from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> <i>bec</i>, which itself comes from the Latin <i>beccus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merriam-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy">Anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chouette_cr%C3%A2ne_(2).jpg"><img alt="an owl's skull with the beak attached" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5095" data-file-width="7143" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Chouette_cr%C3%A2ne_%282%29.jpg/220px-Chouette_cr%C3%A2ne_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Chouette_cr%C3%A2ne_%282%29.jpg/330px-Chouette_cr%C3%A2ne_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Chouette_cr%C3%A2ne_%282%29.jpg/440px-Chouette_cr%C3%A2ne_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chouette_cr%C3%A2ne_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The bony core of the beak is a lightweight framework, like that seen on this <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owl</a>'s skull.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although beaks vary significantly in size and shape from species to species, their underlying structures have a similar pattern. All beaks are composed of two jaws, generally known as the upper mandible (or maxilla) and lower mandible (or mandible).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coues_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coues-4">[4]</a></sup> The upper, and in some cases the lower, mandibles are strengthened internally by a complex <a href="/wiki/Three-dimensional_space" title="Three-dimensional space">three-dimensional</a> network of bony spicules (or <a href="/wiki/Trabecula" title="Trabecula">trabeculae</a>) seated in soft <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a> and surrounded by the hard outer layers of the beak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill149_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gill149-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seki_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seki-6">[6]</a></sup> The avian jaw apparatus is made up of two units: one <a href="/wiki/Four-bar_linkage" title="Four-bar linkage">four-bar linkage</a> mechanism and one five-bar linkage mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mandibles">Mandibles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mandibles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gull_Beak_IMG_1575.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gull_Beak_IMG_1575.JPG/220px-Gull_Beak_IMG_1575.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gull_Beak_IMG_1575.JPG/330px-Gull_Beak_IMG_1575.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gull_Beak_IMG_1575.JPG/440px-Gull_Beak_IMG_1575.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gull_Beak_IMG_1575.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A gull's upper mandible can flex upwards because it is supported by small bones which can move slightly backwards and forwards.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The upper mandible is supported by a three-pronged <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bone</a> called the intermaxillary. The upper prong of this bone is embedded into the forehead, while the two lower prongs attach to the sides of the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a>. At the base of the upper mandible a thin sheet of nasal bones is attached to the skull at the nasofrontal hinge, which gives mobility to the upper mandible, allowing it to move upwards and downwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Proctor66_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Proctor66-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PaleognathNeognath.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1755" data-file-width="1644" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/PaleognathNeognath.png/220px-PaleognathNeognath.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/PaleognathNeognath.png/330px-PaleognathNeognath.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/PaleognathNeognath.png/440px-PaleognathNeognath.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PaleognathNeognath.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Position of <a href="/wiki/Vomer" title="Vomer">vomer</a> (shaded red) in neognathae (left) and paleognathae (right)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The base of the upper mandible, or the roof when seen from the mouth, is the palate, the structure of which differs greatly in the <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratites</a>. Here, the <a href="/wiki/Vomer" title="Vomer">vomer</a> is large and connects with premaxillae and maxillopalatine bones in a condition termed as a "paleognathous palate". All other extant birds have a narrow forked vomer that does not connect with other bones and is then termed as neognathous. The shape of these bones varies across the bird families.</p>
<p>The lower mandible is supported by a bone known as the inferior maxillary bone—a compound bone composed of two distinct ossified pieces. These ossified plates (or <a href="/wiki/Ramus_of_the_mandible" title="Ramus of the mandible">rami</a>), which can be U-shaped or V-shaped,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coues_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coues-4">[4]</a></sup> join distally (the exact location of the joint depends on the species) but are separated <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Proximal_and_distal" title="Anatomical terms of location">proximally</a>, attaching on either side of the head to the quadrate bone. The jaw muscles, which allow the bird to close its beak, attach to the proximal end of the lower mandible and to the bird's skull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill148_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gill148-8">[8]</a></sup> The muscles that depress the lower mandible are usually weak, except in a few birds such as the starlings and the extinct <a href="/wiki/Huia" title="Huia">Huia</a>, which have well-developed <a href="/wiki/Digastric_muscle" title="Digastric muscle">digastric muscles</a> that aid in foraging by prying or gaping actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In most birds, these muscles are relatively small as compared to the jaw muscles of similarly sized mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rhamphotheca">Rhamphotheca</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Rhamphotheca">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The outer surface of the beak consists of a thin horny sheath of <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratin</a> called the <b>rhamphotheca</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Proctor66_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Proctor66-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill148_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gill148-8">[8]</a></sup> which can be subdivided into the <b>rhinotheca</b> of the upper mandible and the <b>gnathotheca</b> of the lower mandible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell47_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell47-11">[11]</a></sup> This covering arises from the <a href="/wiki/Malpighian_layer" title="Malpighian layer">Malpighian layer</a> of the bird's <a href="/wiki/Epidermis_(zoology)" title="Epidermis (zoology)">epidermis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell47_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell47-11">[11]</a></sup> growing from plates at the base of each mandible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> There is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vascular" title="Vascular">vascular</a> layer between the rhamphotheca and the deeper layers of the <a href="/wiki/Dermis" title="Dermis">dermis</a>, which is attached directly to the <a href="/wiki/Periosteum" title="Periosteum">periosteum</a> of the bones of the beak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Samour296_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Samour296-13">[13]</a></sup> The rhamphotheca grows continuously in most birds, and in some species, the color varies seasonally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In some <a href="/wiki/Auk" title="Auk">alcids</a>, such as the puffins, parts of the rhamphotheca are shed each year after the breeding season, while some pelicans shed a part of the bill called a "bill horn" that develops in the breeding season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>While most <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/extant" title="wiktionary:extant">extant</a> birds have a single seamless rhamphotheca, species in a few families, including the <a href="/wiki/Albatross" title="Albatross">albatrosses</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell47_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell47-11">[11]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Emu" title="Emu">emu</a>, have compound rhamphothecae that consist of several pieces separated and defined by softer keratinous grooves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Studies have shown that this was the primitive ancestral state of the rhamphotheca, and that the modern simple rhamphotheca resulted from the gradual loss of the defining grooves through evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tomia">Tomia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Tomia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mergus_merganser_-Hogganfield_Loch,_Glasgow,_Scotland_-female-8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Mergus_merganser_-Hogganfield_Loch%2C_Glasgow%2C_Scotland_-female-8.jpg/220px-Mergus_merganser_-Hogganfield_Loch%2C_Glasgow%2C_Scotland_-female-8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Mergus_merganser_-Hogganfield_Loch%2C_Glasgow%2C_Scotland_-female-8.jpg/330px-Mergus_merganser_-Hogganfield_Loch%2C_Glasgow%2C_Scotland_-female-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Mergus_merganser_-Hogganfield_Loch%2C_Glasgow%2C_Scotland_-female-8.jpg/440px-Mergus_merganser_-Hogganfield_Loch%2C_Glasgow%2C_Scotland_-female-8.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mergus_merganser_-Hogganfield_Loch,_Glasgow,_Scotland_-female-8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The sawtooth serrations on a <a href="/wiki/Common_merganser" title="Common merganser">common merganser</a>'s bill help it to hold tight to its fish prey.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>tomia</b> (singular <i>tomium</i>) are the cutting edges of the two mandibles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In most birds, these range from rounded to slightly sharp, but some species have evolved structural modifications that allow them to handle their typical food sources better.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stettenheim_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stettenheim-21">[21]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Granivorous" title="Granivorous">Granivorous</a> (seed-eating) birds, for example, have ridges in their tomia, which help the bird to slice through a seed's outer <a href="/wiki/Husk" title="Husk">hull</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Most <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcons</a> have a sharp projection along the upper mandible, with a corresponding notch on the lower mandible. They use this "tooth" to sever their prey's vertebrae fatally or to rip insects apart. Some <a href="/wiki/Kite_(bird)" title="Kite (bird)">kites</a>, principally those that prey on insects or lizards, also have one or more of these sharp projections,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> as do the <a href="/wiki/Shrike" title="Shrike">shrikes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Some fish-eating species, e.g., the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merganser" title="Merganser">mergansers</a>, have sawtooth serrations along their tomia, which help them to keep hold of their slippery, wriggling prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Birds in roughly 30 families have tomia lined with tight bunches of very short bristles along their entire length. Most of these species are either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insectivorous" title="Insectivorous">insectivores</a> (preferring hard-shelled prey) or <a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snail</a> eaters, and the brush-like projections may help to increase the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_friction" title="Coefficient of friction">coefficient of friction</a> between the mandibles, thereby improving the bird's ability to hold hard prey items.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Serrations on <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">hummingbird</a> bills, found in 23% of all hummingbird genera, may perform a similar function, allowing the birds to effectively hold insect prey. They may also allow shorter-billed hummingbirds to function as <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a> thieves, as they can more effectively hold and cut through long or waxy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corolla_(flower)" title="Corolla (flower)">flower corollas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In some cases, the color of a bird's tomia can help to distinguish between similar species. The <a href="/wiki/Snow_goose" title="Snow goose">snow goose</a>, for example, has a reddish-pink bill with black tomia, while the whole beak of the similar <a href="/wiki/Ross%27s_goose" title="Ross's goose">Ross's goose</a> is pinkish-red, without darker tomia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culmen">Culmen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Culmen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MeasureBeakThrush.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1017" data-file-width="1400" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/MeasureBeakThrush.jpg/220px-MeasureBeakThrush.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/MeasureBeakThrush.jpg/330px-MeasureBeakThrush.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/MeasureBeakThrush.jpg/440px-MeasureBeakThrush.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MeasureBeakThrush.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A thrush's culmen is measured in a straight line from the tip of the beak to a set point — here, where the feathering starts on the bird's forehead.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>culmen</b> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorsum_(anatomy)" title="Dorsum (anatomy)">dorsal</a> ridge of the upper mandible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Likened by ornithologist <a href="/wiki/Elliott_Coues" title="Elliott Coues">Elliott Coues</a> to the ridge line of a roof, it is the "highest middle lengthwise line of the bill" and runs from the point where the upper mandible emerges from the forehead's feathers to its tip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The bill's length along the culmen is one of the regular measurements made during <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">bird banding (ringing)</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pyle_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pyle-31">[31]</a></sup> and is particularly useful in feeding studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borras_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borras-32">[32]</a></sup> There are several standard measurements that can be made—from the beak's tip to the point where feathering starts on the forehead, from the tip to the anterior edge of the nostrils, from the tip to the base of the skull, or from the tip to the cere (for raptors and owls)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>—and scientists from various parts of the world generally favor one method over another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borras_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Borras-32">[32]</a></sup> In all cases, these are <a href="/wiki/Chord_(geometry)" title="Chord (geometry)">chord measurements</a> (measured in a straight line from point to point, ignoring any curve in the culmen) taken with <a href="/wiki/Calipers" title="Calipers">calipers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pyle_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pyle-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The shape or color of the culmen can also help with the identification of birds in the field. For example, the culmen of the <a href="/wiki/Parrot_crossbill" title="Parrot crossbill">parrot crossbill</a> is strongly decurved, while that of the very similar <a href="/wiki/Red_crossbill" title="Red crossbill">red crossbill</a> is more moderately curved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The culmen of a juvenile <a href="/wiki/Common_loon" title="Common loon">common loon</a> is all dark, while that of the very similarly <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumaged</a> juvenile <a href="/wiki/Yellow-billed_loon" title="Yellow-billed loon">yellow-billed loon</a> is pale towards the tip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gonys">Gonys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Gonys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <b>gonys</b> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventral" title="Ventral">ventral</a> ridge of the lower mandible, created by the junction of the bone's two rami, or lateral plates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The proximal end of that junction—where the two plates separate—is known as the <b>gonydeal angle</b> or <b>gonydeal expansion</b>. In some gull species, the plates expand slightly at that point, creating a noticeable bulge; the size and shape of the gonydeal angle can be useful in identifying between otherwise similar species. Adults of many species of large gulls have a reddish or orangish <b>gonydeal spot</b> near the gonydeal expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howell23_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howell23-37">[37]</a></sup> This spot triggers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Begging_behavior_in_animals" title="Begging behavior in animals">begging behavior</a> in gull chicks. The chick pecks at the spot on its parent's bill, which in turn stimulates the parent to regurgitate food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commissure">Commissure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Commissure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Depending on its usage, <i>commissure</i> may refer to the junction of the upper and lower mandibles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> or alternately, to the full-length apposition of the closed mandibles, from the corners of the mouth to the tip of the beak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gape">Gape</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gape">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_fledgeling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Sturnus_vulgaris_fledgeling.jpg/220px-Sturnus_vulgaris_fledgeling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Sturnus_vulgaris_fledgeling.jpg/330px-Sturnus_vulgaris_fledgeling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Sturnus_vulgaris_fledgeling.jpg/440px-Sturnus_vulgaris_fledgeling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_fledgeling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A young <a href="/wiki/Starling" title="Starling">starling</a> with a bright yellow gape</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Bird_anatomy" title="Bird anatomy">bird anatomy</a>, the <b>gape</b> is the interior of the open mouth of a bird, and the <b>gape flange</b> is the region where the two <a href="/wiki/Mandible" title="Mandible">mandibles</a> join together at the base of the beak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The width of the gape can be a factor in the choice of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/House_sparrow_portrait.jpg/220px-House_sparrow_portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/House_sparrow_portrait.jpg/330px-House_sparrow_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/House_sparrow_portrait.jpg/440px-House_sparrow_portrait.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow_portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The gape flange on this juvenile <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house sparrow</a> is the yellowish region at the base of the beak.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gapes of juvenile <a href="/wiki/Altricial" title="Altricial">altricial</a> birds are often brightly coloured, sometimes with contrasting spots or other patterns, and these are believed to be an indication of their health, fitness and competitive ability. Based on this, the parents decide how to distribute food among the chicks in the nest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Some species, especially in the families <a href="/wiki/Viduidae" title="Viduidae">Viduidae</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estrildidae" title="Estrildidae">Estrildidae</a>, have bright spots on the gape known as gape tubercles or gape papillae. These nodular spots are conspicuous even in low light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> A study examining the nestling gapes of eight <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerine</a> species found that the gapes were conspicuous in the <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> spectrum (visible to birds but not to humans).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Parents may, however, not rely solely on the gape coloration, and other factors influencing their decision remain unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Red gape color has been shown in several experiments to induce feeding. An experiment in manipulating brood size and immune system with <a href="/wiki/Barn_swallow" title="Barn swallow">barn swallow</a> nestlings showed the vividness of the gape was positively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Correlation" title="Correlation">correlated</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-cell" title="T-cell">T-cell</a>–mediated <a href="/wiki/Immunocompetence" title="Immunocompetence">immunocompetence</a>, and that larger brood size and injection with an <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigen</a> led to a less vivid gape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saino03_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saino03-47">[47]</a></sup> Conversely, the red gape of the <a href="/wiki/Common_cuckoo" title="Common cuckoo">common cuckoo</a> (<i>Cuculus canorus</i>) did not induce extra feeding in host parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">brood parasites,</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Hodgson%27s_hawk-cuckoo" title="Hodgson's hawk-cuckoo">Hodgson's hawk-cuckoo</a> (<i>C. fugax</i>), have colored patches on the wing that mimic the gape color of the parasitized species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>When born, the chick's gape flanges are fleshy. As it grows into a <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledgling</a>, the gape flanges remain somewhat swollen and can thus be used to recognize that a particular bird is young.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> By the time it reaches adulthood, the gape flanges will no longer be visible.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nares">Nares</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nares">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saker_Falcon_profile_shot.jpg"><img alt="Head of a black and white bird with a large dark eye. Its hooked beak is gray with a black tip and its round nostril has a small lump in the center." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2117" data-file-width="2870" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Saker_Falcon_profile_shot.jpg/220px-Saker_Falcon_profile_shot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Saker_Falcon_profile_shot.jpg/330px-Saker_Falcon_profile_shot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Saker_Falcon_profile_shot.jpg/440px-Saker_Falcon_profile_shot.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saker_Falcon_profile_shot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">Falcons</a> have a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tubercule" title="Tubercule">tubercule</a> within each nare.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most species of birds have external nares (<a href="/wiki/Nostril" title="Nostril">nostrils</a>) located somewhere on their beak. The nares are two holes—circular, oval or slit-like in shape—which lead to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasal_cavities" title="Nasal cavities">nasal cavities</a> within the bird's skull, and thus to the rest of the <a href="/wiki/Bird_anatomy#Respiratory_system" title="Bird anatomy">respiratory system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell375_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell375-51">[51]</a></sup> In most bird species, the nares are located in the basal third of the upper mandible. <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">Kiwis</a> are a notable exception; their nares are located at the tip of their bills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stettenheim_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stettenheim-21">[21]</a></sup> A handful of species have no external nares. <a href="/wiki/Cormorant" title="Cormorant">Cormorants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Darter" title="Darter">darters</a> have primitive external nares as nestlings, but these close soon after the birds <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledge</a>; adults of these species (and <a href="/wiki/Gannet" title="Gannet">gannets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Booby" title="Booby">boobies</a> of all ages, which also lack external nostrils) breathe through their mouths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell47_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell47-11">[11]</a></sup> There is typically a <a href="/wiki/Nasal_septum" title="Nasal septum">septum</a> made of bone or cartilage that separates the two nares, but in some families (including gulls, cranes and New World vultures), the septum is missing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell47_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell47-11">[11]</a></sup> While the nares are uncovered in most species, they are covered with feathers in a few groups of birds, including grouse and ptarmigans, crows, and some woodpeckers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell375_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell375-51">[51]</a></sup> The feathers over a ptarmigan's nostrils help to warm the air it inhales,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> while those over a woodpecker's nares help to keep wood particles from clogging its nasal passages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Species in the bird order <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Procellariformes" title="Procellariformes">Procellariformes</a> have nostrils enclosed in double tubes which sit atop or along the sides of the upper mandible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell375_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell375-51">[51]</a></sup> These species, which include the albatrosses, petrels, diving petrels, storm petrels, fulmars and shearwaters, are widely known as "tubenoses".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> A number of species, including the <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcons</a>, have a small bony <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tubercule" title="Tubercule">tubercule</a> which projects from their nares. The function of this tubercule is unknown. Some scientists suggest it may act as a baffle, slowing down or diffusing airflow into the nares (and thus allowing the bird to continue breathing without damaging its respiratory system) during high-speed dives, but this theory has not been proved experimentally. Not all species that fly at high speeds have such tubercules, while some species which fly at low speeds do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operculum">Operculum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Operculum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pigeon_portrait_4861.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="1600" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Pigeon_portrait_4861.jpg/220px-Pigeon_portrait_4861.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Pigeon_portrait_4861.jpg/330px-Pigeon_portrait_4861.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Pigeon_portrait_4861.jpg/440px-Pigeon_portrait_4861.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pigeon_portrait_4861.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Rock_dove" title="Rock dove">rock dove</a>'s operculum is a mass at the base of the bill.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The nares of some birds are covered by an <b>operculum</b> (plural <i>opercula</i>), a membraneous, horny or <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilaginous</a> flap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill117_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gill117-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In diving birds, the operculum keeps water out of the nasal cavity;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill117_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gill117-56">[56]</a></sup> when the birds dive, the impact force of the water closes the operculum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Some species which feed on flowers have opercula to help to keep pollen from clogging their nasal passages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill117_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gill117-56">[56]</a></sup> while the opercula of the two species of <a href="/wiki/Attagis" title="Attagis"><i>Attagis</i> seedsnipe</a> help to keep dust out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The nares of nestling <a href="/wiki/Tawny_frogmouth" title="Tawny frogmouth">tawny frogmouths</a> are covered with large dome-shaped opercula, which help to reduce the rapid evaporation of water vapor, and may also help to increase condensation within the nostrils themselves—both critical functions, since the nestlings get fluids only from the food their parents bring them. These opercula shrink as the birds age, disappearing completely by the time they reach adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pigeon" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a>, the operculum has evolved into a soft swollen mass that sits at the base of the bill, above the nares;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> though it is sometimes referred to as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cere" title="Cere">cere</a></i>, this is a different structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tapaculo" title="Tapaculo">Tapaculos</a> are the only birds able to move their opercula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell375_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell375-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rosette">Rosette</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Rosette">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Puffin" title="Puffin">puffin</a>, have a fleshy rosette, sometimes called a "gape rosette",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> at the corners of the beak. In the puffin, this is grown as part of its display plumage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cere">Cere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Birds from a handful of families—including raptors, owls, skuas, parrots, turkeys and curassows—have a waxy structure called a <b>cere</b> (from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_(language)" title="Latin (language)">Latin</a> <i>cera</i>, which means "wax") which covers the base of their bill. This structure typically contains the nares, except in the owls, where the nares are distal to the cere. Although it is sometimes feathered in parrots,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> the cere is typically bare and often brightly colored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stettenheim_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stettenheim-21">[21]</a></sup> In raptors, the cere is a sexual signal which indicates the "quality" of a bird; the orangeness of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montague%27s_harrier" title="Montague's harrier">Montague's harrier</a>'s cere, for example, correlates to its body mass and physical condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mougeo_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mougeo-66">[66]</a></sup> The cere color of young <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurasian_scops-owl" title="Eurasian scops-owl">Eurasian scops-owls</a> has an <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> (UV) component, with a UV peak that correlates to the bird's mass. A chick with a lower body mass has a UV peak at a higher <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> than a chick with a higher body mass does. Studies have shown that parent owls preferentially feed chicks with ceres that show higher wavelength UV peaks, that is, lighter-weight chicks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The color or appearance of the cere can be used to distinguish between males and females in some species. For example, the male <a href="/wiki/Great_curassow" title="Great curassow">great curassow</a> has a yellow cere, which the female (and young males) lack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The male <a href="/wiki/Budgerigar" title="Budgerigar">budgerigar</a>'s cere is blue, while the female's is pinkish or brown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nail">Nail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Nail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:London_MMB_49_Ruislip_Lido.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2400" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/London_MMB_49_Ruislip_Lido.jpg/220px-London_MMB_49_Ruislip_Lido.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/London_MMB_49_Ruislip_Lido.jpg/330px-London_MMB_49_Ruislip_Lido.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/London_MMB_49_Ruislip_Lido.jpg/440px-London_MMB_49_Ruislip_Lido.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:London_MMB_49_Ruislip_Lido.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The nail is the black tip of this <a href="/wiki/Mute_swan" title="Mute swan">mute swan</a>'s beak.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All birds of the family <a href="/wiki/Anatidae" title="Anatidae">Anatidae</a> (ducks, geese, and swans) have a <b>nail</b>, a plate of hard horny tissue at the tip of the beak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Shield" title="Shield">shield</a>-shaped structure, which sometimes spans the entire width of the beak, is often bent at the tip to form a hook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elliot_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elliot-71">[71]</a></sup> It serves different purposes depending on the bird's primary food source. Most species use their nails to dig seeds out of mud or vegetation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Diving_duck" title="Diving duck">diving ducks</a> use theirs to pry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a> from rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> There is evidence that the nail may help a bird to grasp things; species which use strong grasping motions to secure their food (such as when catching and holding onto a large squirming frog) have very wide nails.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Certain types of <a href="/wiki/Mechanoreceptor" title="Mechanoreceptor">mechanoreceptors</a>, nerve cells that are sensitive to pressure, vibration or touch, are located under the nail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>The shape or color of the nail can sometimes be used to help distinguish between similar-looking species or between various ages of waterfowl. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Greater_scaup" title="Greater scaup">greater scaup</a> has a wider black nail than does the very similar <a href="/wiki/Lesser_scaup" title="Lesser scaup">lesser scaup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Juvenile "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grey_goose" title="Grey goose">grey geese</a>" have dark nails, while most adults have pale nails.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The nail gave the wildfowl family one of its former names: "Unguirostres" comes from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_(language)" title="Latin (language)">Latin</a> <i>ungus</i>, meaning "nail" and <i>rostrum</i>, meaning "beak".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elliot_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elliot-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rictal_bristles">Rictal bristles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Rictal bristles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Rictal bristles are stiff hair-like feathers that arise around the base of the beak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lederer_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lederer-78">[78]</a></sup> They are common among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insectivorous" title="Insectivorous">insectivorous</a> birds, but are also found in some non-insectivorous species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conover_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conover-79">[79]</a></sup> Their function is uncertain, although several possibilities have been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lederer_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lederer-78">[78]</a></sup> They may function as a "net", helping in the capture of flying prey, although to date, there has been no <a href="/wiki/Empirical_evidence" title="Empirical evidence">empirical evidence</a> to support this idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunningham_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cunningham-80">[80]</a></sup> There is some experimental evidence to suggest that they may prevent particles from striking the eyes if, for example, a prey item is missed or broken apart on contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conover_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Conover-79">[79]</a></sup> They may also help to protect the eyes from particles encountered in flight, or from casual contact from vegetation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunningham_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cunningham-80">[80]</a></sup> There is also evidence that the rictal bristles of some species may function tactilely, in a manner similar to that of mammal whiskers (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrissae" title="Vibrissae">vibrissae</a>). Studies have shown that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbst_corpuscle" title="Herbst corpuscle">Herbst corpuscles</a>, mechanoreceptors sensitive to pressure and vibration, are found in association with rictal bristles. They may help with prey detection, with navigation in darkened nest cavities, with the gathering of information during flight or with prey handling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunningham_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cunningham-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Egg_tooth">Egg tooth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Egg tooth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_Tern_chick-4c.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1364" data-file-width="1676" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Arctic_Tern_chick-4c.jpg/220px-Arctic_Tern_chick-4c.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Arctic_Tern_chick-4c.jpg/330px-Arctic_Tern_chick-4c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Arctic_Tern_chick-4c.jpg/440px-Arctic_Tern_chick-4c.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_Tern_chick-4c.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This <a href="/wiki/Arctic_tern" title="Arctic tern">Arctic tern</a> chick still has its egg tooth, the small white projection near the tip of its upper mandible.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Egg_tooth" title="Egg tooth">Egg tooth</a></div>
<p>Full-term chicks of most bird species have a small sharp, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcified" title="Calcified">calcified</a> projection on their beak, which they use to chip their way out of their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">egg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell178_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell178-81">[81]</a></sup> Commonly known as an egg tooth, this white spike is generally near the tip of the upper mandible, though some species have one near the tip of their lower mandible instead, and a few species have one on each mandible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perrins_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perrins-82">[82]</a></sup> Despite its name, the projection is not an actual <a href="/wiki/Tooth" title="Tooth">tooth</a>, as the similarly-named projections of some <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> are; instead, it is part of the <a href="/wiki/Integumentary_system" title="Integumentary system">integumentary system</a>, as are <a href="/wiki/Claw" title="Claw">claws</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scale_(zoology)" title="Scale (zoology)">scales</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The hatching chick first uses its egg tooth to break the membrane around an air chamber at the wide end of the egg. Then it pecks at the eggshell while turning slowly within the egg, eventually (over a period of hours or days) creating a series of small circular fractures in the shell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill427_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gill427-84">[84]</a></sup> Once it has breached the egg's surface, the chick continues to chip at it until it has made a large hole. The weakened egg eventually shatters under the pressure of the bird's movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill428_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gill428-85">[85]</a></sup> The egg tooth is so critical to a successful escape from the egg that chicks of most species will perish unhatched if they fail to develop one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perrins_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Perrins-82">[82]</a></sup> However, there are a few species which do not have egg teeth. <a href="/wiki/Megapode" title="Megapode">Megapode</a> chicks have an egg tooth while still in the egg but lose it before hatching,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill427_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gill427-84">[84]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">kiwi</a> chicks never develop one; chicks of both families escape their eggs by kicking their way out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Most chicks lose their egg teeth within a few days of hatching,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell178_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell178-81">[81]</a></sup> though <a href="/wiki/Petrel" title="Petrel">petrels</a> keep theirs for nearly three weeks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill428_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gill428-85">[85]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Marbled_murrelet" title="Marbled murrelet">marbled murrelets</a> have theirs for up to a month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Generally, the egg tooth drops off, though in <a href="/wiki/Songbird" title="Songbird">songbirds</a> it is reabsorbed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill428_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gill428-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Color">Color</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Color">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The color of a bird's beak results from concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigments</a>—primarily <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanins</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carotenoids" title="Carotenoids">carotenoids</a>—in the epidermal layers, including the rhamphotheca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eumelanin" title="Eumelanin">Eumelanin</a>, which is found in the bare parts of many bird species, is responsible for all shades of gray and black; the denser the deposits of pigment found in the epidermis, the darker the resulting color. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phaeomelanin" title="Phaeomelanin">Phaeomelanin</a> produces "earth tones" ranging from gold and rufous to various shades of brown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill62_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hill62-89">[89]</a></sup> Although it is thought to occur in combination with eumelanin in beaks which are buff, tan, or horn-colored, researchers have yet to isolate phaeomelanin from any beak structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> More than a dozen types of carotenoids are responsible for the coloration of most red, orange, and yellow beaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hue" title="Hue">hue</a> of the color is determined by the precise mix of red and yellow pigments, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturation_(color_theory)" title="Saturation (color theory)">saturation</a> is determined by the density of the deposited pigments. For example, bright red is created by dense deposits of mostly red pigments, while dull yellow is created by diffuse deposits of mostly yellow pigments. Bright orange is created by dense deposits of both red and yellow pigments, in roughly equal concentrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Beak coloration helps to make displays using those beaks more obvious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Birds are capable of <a href="/wiki/Bird_vision" title="Bird vision">seeing</a> colors in the <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> range, and some species are known to have ultraviolet peaks of reflectance (indicating the presence of ultraviolet color) on their beaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jouventin_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jouventin-94">[94]</a></sup> The presence and intensity of these peaks may indicate a bird's fitness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mougeo_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mougeo-66">[66]</a></sup> sexual maturity or pair bond status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jouventin_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jouventin-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/King_penguin" title="King penguin">King</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">emperor penguins</a>, for example, show spots of ultraviolet reflectance only as adults. These spots are brighter on paired birds than on courting birds. The position of such spots on the beak may be important in allowing birds to identify <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conspecific" title="Conspecific">conspecifics</a>. For instance, the very similarly-plumaged king and emperor penguins have UV-reflective spots in different positions on their beaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jouventin_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jouventin-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>In general, beak color depends on a combination of the bird's <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormonal state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">diet</a>. Colors are typically brightest as the breeding season approaches, and palest after breeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howell23_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Howell23-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dimorphism">Dimorphism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Dimorphism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Huia_Buller.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="920" data-file-width="735" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Huia_Buller.jpg/220px-Huia_Buller.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Huia_Buller.jpg/330px-Huia_Buller.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Huia_Buller.jpg/440px-Huia_Buller.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Huia_Buller.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The beaks of the now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Huia" title="Huia">Huia</a> (female upper, male lower) show marked sexual dimorphism</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The size and shape of the beak can vary across species as well as between them; in some species, the size and proportions of the beak vary between males and females. This allows the sexes to utilize different ecological niches, thereby reducing <a href="/wiki/Intraspecific_competition" title="Intraspecific competition">intraspecific competition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> For example, females of nearly all shorebirds have longer bills than males of the same species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> and female <a href="/wiki/American_avocet" title="American avocet">American avocets</a> have beaks which are slightly more upturned than those of males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Males of the larger gull species have bigger, stouter beaks than those of females of the same species, and immatures can have smaller, more slender beaks than those of adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Hornbill" title="Hornbill">hornbills</a> show <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a> in the size and shape of both beaks and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Casque_(anatomy)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Casque (anatomy) (page does not exist)">casques</a>, and the female <a href="/wiki/Huia" title="Huia">huia</a>'s slim, decurved bill was nearly twice as long as the male's straight, thicker one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Color can also differ between sexes or ages within a species. Typically, such a color difference is due to the presence of <a href="/wiki/Androgen" title="Androgen">androgens</a>. For example, in <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house sparrows</a>, melanins are produced only in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">testosterone</a>; <a href="/wiki/Castration" title="Castration">castrated</a> male house sparrows—like female house sparrows—have brown beaks. Castration also prevents the normal seasonal color change in the beaks of male <a href="/wiki/Black-headed_gull" title="Black-headed gull">black-headed gulls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indigo_bunting" title="Indigo bunting">indigo buntings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Functions">Functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyto_alba_-USA_-three_hissing_and_clicking-8.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Three <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a> threatening an intruder. Barn owl threat displays usually include hissing and bill-snapping, as here</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Birds may bite or stab with their beaks to defend themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Some species use their beaks in displays of various sorts. As part of his courtship, for example, the male <a href="/wiki/Garganey" title="Garganey">garganey</a> touches his beak to the blue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speculum_feather" title="Speculum feather">speculum feathers</a> on his wings in a fake preening display, and the male <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_duck" title="Mandarin duck">Mandarin duck</a> does the same with his orange sail feathers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> A number of species use a gaping, open beak in their fear and/or threat displays. Some augment the display by hissing or breathing heavily, while others clap their beaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">Communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A number of species, including <a href="/wiki/Stork" title="Stork">storks</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frogmouth" title="Frogmouth">frogmouths</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Noisy_miner" title="Noisy miner">noisy miner</a>, use bill clapping as a form of communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heat_exchange">Heat exchange</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Heat exchange">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Studies have shown that some birds use their beaks to rid themselves of excess heat. The <a href="/wiki/Toco_toucan" title="Toco toucan">toco toucan</a>, which has the largest beak relative to the size of its body of any bird species, is capable of modifying the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_flow" title="Blood flow">blood flow</a> to its beak. This process allows the beak to work as a "transient thermal radiator", reportedly rivaling an <a href="/wiki/Elephant#Ears" title="Elephant">elephant's ears</a> in its ability to radiate body heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Measurements of the bill sizes of several species of <a href="/wiki/American_sparrow" title="American sparrow">American sparrows</a> found in <a href="/wiki/Salt_marsh" title="Salt marsh">salt marshes</a> along the North American coastlines show a strong correlation with summer temperatures recorded in the locations where the sparrows breed; <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> alone showed a much weaker correlation. By dumping excess heat through their bills, the sparrows are able to avoid the water loss which would be required by evaporative cooling—an important benefit in a windy habitat where freshwater is scarce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Several <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratites</a>, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_ostrich" title="Common ostrich">common ostrich</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Emu" title="Emu">emu</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_cassowary" title="Southern cassowary">southern cassowary</a>, use various bare parts of their bodies (including their beaks) to dissipate as much as 40% of their metabolic heat production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Alternately, studies have shown that birds from colder climates (higher altitudes or latitudes and lower environmental temperatures) have smaller beaks, lessening heat loss from that structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Billing">Billing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Billing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Morus_bassanus_billing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="517" data-file-width="775" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Morus_bassanus_billing.jpg/220px-Morus_bassanus_billing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Morus_bassanus_billing.jpg/330px-Morus_bassanus_billing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Morus_bassanus_billing.jpg/440px-Morus_bassanus_billing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Morus_bassanus_billing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
When billing, <a href="/wiki/Northern_gannet" title="Northern gannet">northern gannets</a> raise their beaks high and clatter them against each other.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During courtship, mated pairs of many bird species touch or clasp each other's bills. Termed <b>billing</b> (also <b>nebbing</b> in British English),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> this behavior appears to strengthen <a href="/wiki/Pair_bond" title="Pair bond">pair bonding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The amount of contact involved varies among species. Some gently touch only a part of their partner's beak while others clash their beaks vigorously together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gannet" title="Gannet">Gannets</a> raise their bills high and repeatedly clatter them, the male <a href="/wiki/Puffin" title="Puffin">puffin</a> nibbles at the female's beak, the male <a href="/wiki/Waxwing" title="Waxwing">waxwing</a> puts his bill in the female's mouth and <a href="/wiki/Raven" title="Raven">ravens</a> hold each other's beaks in a prolonged "kiss".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Billing can also be used as a gesture of appeasement or subordination. Subordinate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gray_jay" title="Gray jay">gray jay</a> routinely bill more dominant birds, lowering their body and quivering their wings in the manner of a young bird food begging as they do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> A number of parasites, including <a href="/wiki/Rhinonyssidae" title="Rhinonyssidae">rhinonyssids</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Trichomonas_gallinae" title="Trichomonas gallinae">Trichomonas gallinae</a></i> are known to be transferred between birds during episodes of billing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>Usage of the term has spread beyond avian behavior; "billing and cooing" in reference to human courtship (particularly kissing) has been in use since <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare's</a> time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> and derives from the courtship of doves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beak_trimming">Beak trimming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Beak trimming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Debeaking" title="Debeaking">Debeaking</a></div>
<p>Because the beak is a sensitive organ with many sensory receptors, beak trimming (sometimes referred to as 'debeaking') is "acutely painful"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grandin_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grandin-118">[118]</a></sup> to the birds it is performed on. It is nonetheless routinely done to intensively farmed <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a> flocks, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Layer_hen" title="Layer hen">laying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Broiler" title="Broiler">broiler</a> breeder flocks, because it helps reduce the damage the flocks inflict on themselves due to a number of <a href="/wiki/Stress_(biology)" title="Stress (biology)">stress</a>-induced behaviors, including <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism_(zoology)" title="Cannibalism (zoology)">cannibalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vent_pecking" title="Vent pecking">vent pecking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feather_pecking" title="Feather pecking">feather pecking</a>. A cauterizing blade or infrared beam is used to cut off about half of the upper beak and about a third of the lower beak. Pain and sensitivity can persist for weeks or months after the procedure, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuromas" title="Neuromas">neuromas</a> can form along the cut edges. Food intake typically decreases for some period after the beak is trimmed. However, studies show that trimmed poultry's <a href="/wiki/Adrenal_gland" title="Adrenal gland">adrenal glands</a> weigh less, and their plasma <a href="/wiki/Corticosterone" title="Corticosterone">corticosterone</a> levels are lower than those found in untrimmed poultry, indicating that they are less stressed overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grandin_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grandin-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>A similar but separate practice, usually performed by an <a href="/wiki/Avian_veterinarian" title="Avian veterinarian">avian veterinarian</a> or an experienced birdkeeper, involves clipping, filing or sanding the beaks of captive birds for health purposes – in order to correct or temporarily alleviate overgrowths or deformities and better allow the bird to go about its normal feeding and preening activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fostersmith_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-fostersmith-119">[119]</a></sup> Amongst <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">raptor</a> keepers, this practice is commonly known as "coping".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ash_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-ash-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bill_tip_organ">Bill tip organ</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Beak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Bill tip organ">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <b>bill tip organ</b> is a region found near the tip of the bill in several types of birds that forage particularly by probing. The region has a high density of nerve endings known as the <a href="/wiki/Corpuscles_of_Herbst" title="Corpuscles of Herbst">corpuscles of Herbst</a>. This consists of pits in the bill surface which in the living bird is occupied by cells that sense pressure changes. The assumption is that this allows the bird to perform 'remote touch', which means that it can detect movements of animals which the bird does not directly touch. Bird species known to have a 'bill-tip organ' includes members of <a href="/wiki/Ibis" title="Ibis">ibisis</a>, shorebirds of the family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scolopacidae" title="Scolopacidae">Scolopacidae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">kiwis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is a suggestion that across these species, the bill tip organ is more well developed among species foraging in wet habitats (water column or soft mud) than in species using a more terrestrial foraging. However, it has been described in terrestrial birds too, including <a href="/wiki/Parrot" title="Parrot">parrots</a>, who are known for their dextrous extractive foraging techniques. Unlike probing foragers, the tactile pits in parrots are embedded in the hard <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratin</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Beak#Rhamphotheca" title="Beak">rhamphotheca</a>) of the bill, rather than the bone, and along the inner edges of the curved bill, rather than being on the outside of the bill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
